Title: Which Algorithms Can Graph Neural Networks Learn?

URL Source: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2602.13106v1

Published Time: Mon, 16 Feb 2026 02:12:53 GMT

Number of Pages: 96

Markdown Content:
# Which Algorithms Can Graph Neural Networks Learn? 

# Solveig Wittig вИЧ1, Antonis Vasileiou вИЧ1, Robert R. Nerem вИЧ2, Timo Stoll 1,Floris Geerts 3, Yusu Wang 2, and Christopher Morris 1

> 1

RWTH Aachen University 

> 2

University of California San Diego 

> 3

University of Antwerp 

In recent years, there has been growing interest in understanding neural architec-turesвАЩ ability to learn to execute discrete algorithms, a line of work often referred to as neural algorithmic reasoning. The goal is to integrate algorithmic reasoning capabilities into larger neural pipelines. Many such architectures are based on (message-passing) graph neural networks (MPNNs), owing to their permutation equivariance and ability to deal with sparsity and variable-sized inputs. However, existing work is either largely empirical and lacks formal guarantees or it focuses solely on expressivity, leaving open the question of when and how such architectures generalize beyond a finite training set. In this work, we propose a general theoretical framework that characterizes the sufficient conditions under which MPNNs can learn an algorithm from a training set of small instances and provably approximate its behavior on inputs of arbitrary size. Our framework applies to a broad class of algorithms, including single-source shortest paths, minimum spanning trees, and general dynamic programming problems, such as the 0-1 knapsack problem. In addition, we establish impossibility results for a wide range of algorithmic tasks, showing that standard MPNNs cannot learn them, and we derive more expressive MPNN-like architectures that overcome these limitations. Finally, we refine our analysis for the BellmanвАУFord algorithm, yielding a substantially smaller required training set and significantly extending the recent work of Nerem et al. [2025] by allowing for a differentiable regularization loss. Empirical results largely support our theoretical findings.

# 1. Introduction 

Graph neural networks (GNNs), and in particular message-passing graph neural networks 

(MPNNs), constitute a versatile and expressive class of neural architectures for learning over graph-structured data [Gilmer et al., 2017, Scarselli et al., 2009]. Their permutation equivariance and ability to operate on sparse and variable-sized inputs have made them a central tool across a wide range of applications, spanning drug design [Wong et al., 2023], global medium-range weather forecasting [Lam et al., 2023], and combinatorial optimization [Cappart et al., 2023, Gasse et al., 2019, Qian et al., 2024, Scavuzzo et al., 2024]. Recently, MPNNs have played a central role in neural algorithmic reasoning (NAR), a research direction that seeks to bridge classical algorithm design and neural computation [Cappart et al., 2023, Velickovic and Blundell, 2021, Xu et al., 2020]. The goal of NAR is to enable neural networks to learn, execute, and generalize discrete algorithms, thereby seamlessly integrating algorithmic reasoning into end-to-end trainable neural pipelines. Due to their close correspondence with iterative, local graph computation, MPNNs have emerged as a natural architectural backbone for learning graph algorithms such as shortest paths, minimum spanning trees, and dynamic programming procedures [Velickovic et al., 2020]. Despite substantial empirical progress, existing results on neural algorithmic reasoning remain theoretically limited. Most prior work is either purely empirical, demonstrating performance on benchmark instances without formal guarantees, or focuses on existential expressivity questions, 2characterizing which algorithms can in principle be represented by a given architecture, e.g., Az-izian and Lelarge [2021], Chen et al. [2020], He and Vitercik [2025], Loukas [2020], Morris et al. [2019], Pellizzoni et al. [2025], Xu et al. [2019], with a recent focus on transformer architectures, e.g., de Luca and Fountoulakis [2024], de Luca et al. [2025], Merrill and Sabharwal [2025], Sanford et al. [2024,], Yehudai et al. [2025], Zhou et al. [2024]. Such analyses typically abstract away the learning process and provide little insight into sample efficiency or generalization beyond the training distribution. As a consequence, they do not explain when learned models provably generalize to larger or structurally different graphs, a property that is essential for meaningful algorithmic deployment. A notable recent exception is the work of Nerem et al. [2025], which provides theoretical guarantees for learning graph algorithms with MPNNs. However, their analysis is restricted to a single algorithm (i.e., BellmanвАУFord) and enforces correctness via a non-differentiable regularization term. These assumptions limit the applicability of the results to broader classes of algorithms and to standard gradient-based learning pipelines. 

Present work In this work, we develop a general theoretical framework for learning algorithms with MPNNs that addresses these limitations. We characterize sufficient conditions under which an MPNN trained on a small set of instances can provably generalize to inputs of arbitrary size, covering a broad class of graph algorithms while remaining compatible with fully differentiable training objectives. Our framework clarifies which algorithms MPNNs can learn from finite data and which they cannot learn . Concretely, our contributions are as follows. 1. We introduce a theoretical framework characterizing when standard MPNNs (or more expressive MPNNs) can learn the cost function of graph algorithms, uniformly over graphs of arbitrary size, from finite data by minimizing an empirical loss; see Section 3. 2. Using this framework, we identify conditions under which GNNs can learn standard graph algorithms, including single-source shortest-path, minimum spanning tree, and dynamic programming algorithms. 3. For single-source shortest-path, we propose a differentiable вДУ1-regularization term that reduces required training data by balancing empirical risk minimization with regularization to enforce a sparsity pattern; see Section 3.3. 4. We empirically validate that these insights translate into practice, underscoring the role of training data and the proposed regularization; see Section 6. 

Taken together, our framework provides a precise characterization of which algorithms standard and more expressive MPNNs can learn, enabling a more principled understanding of the capabilities and limitations of MPNN-based, data-driven algorithmic design. 

1.1. Related work 

In the following, we discuss related work. 

MPNNs MPNNs [Gilmer et al., 2017, Scarselli et al., 2009] have emerged as the most prominent graph machine learning architecture. Notable instances of this architecture include, e.g., Duvenaud et al. [2015], Hamilton et al. [2017], Kipf and Welling [2017] and VeliƒНkoviƒЗ et al. [2018], which can be subsumed under the message-passing framework introduced in Gilmer et al. 3[2017]. In parallel, approaches based on spectral information were introduced in, e.g., Bruna et al. [2014], Defferrard et al. [2016], Gama et al. [2019], Kipf and Welling [2017], Levie et al. [2019], and Monti et al. [2017]вАФall of which descend from early work in Baskin et al. [1997], Goller and K√Љchler [1996], Kireev [1995], Merkwirth and Lengauer [2005], Micheli and Sestito [2005], Micheli [2009], Scarselli et al. [2009], and Sperduti and Starita [1997]. 

Expressivity of MPNNs The expressivity of an MPNN is the architectureвАЩs ability to express or approximate different functions over a set of graphs. High expressivity means the neural network can represent many functions over this domain. In the literature, the expressivity of MPNNs is modeled mathematically using two main approaches, i.e., separation power compared to graph isomorphism test Morris [2021] and universal approximation theorems Azizian and Lelarge [2021], Geerts and Reutter [2022]. Works following the first approach study if an MPNN, by choosing appropriate parameters, can distinguish the same pairs of non-isomorphic graphs with a given graph isomorphism test. The most commonly used graph isomorphism test for analyzing the expressive power of MPNNs is the 1-dimensional WeisfeilerвАУLeman algorithm (1-WL ), a well-studied heuristic for the graph isomorphism problem, and its more expressive variants [Cai et al., 1992, Weisfeiler and Leman, 1968, Weisfeiler, 1976]. Here, an MPNN distinguishes two non-isomorphic graphs if it can compute different vectorial representations for the two graphs. Specifically, Morris et al. [2019] and Xu et al. [2019] showed that the 1-WL limits the expressive power of any possible MPNN architecture in distinguishing non-isomorphic graphs. In turn, these results have been generalized to the k-dimensional WeisfeilerвАУLeman algorithm, e.g., Azizian and Lelarge [2021], Geerts [2020], Maron et al. [2019], Morris et al. [2019, 2020, 2022] and (ordered) subgraph GNNs Bevilacqua et al. [2022], Cotta et al. [2021], Li et al. [2020], Qian et al. [2022], Zhang et al. [2023]. Works following the second approach study, which functions can be approximated over the set of graphs arbitrarily closely by an MPNN [Azizian and Lelarge, 2021, B√ґker et al., 2023, Chen et al., 2019, Geerts and Reutter, 2022, Maehara and NT, 2019]. 

Generalization abilities of MPNNs Early work by Scarselli et al. [2018], building on clas-sical learning theory [Karpinski and Macintyre, 1997, Vapnik, 1995], bounded the VapnikвАУ Chervonenkis theory dimension of MPNNs with piecewise polynomial activations on fixed graphs by O(P 2n log n), where P is the number of parameters and n the graphвАЩs order; see also Hammer [2001]. However, their MPNN model differs from modern architectures [Gilmer et al., 2017, DвАЩInverno et al., 2024]. Garg et al. [2020] bounded the empirical Rademacher com-plexity of a simple sum-aggregation MPNN in terms of graph degree, depth, Lipschitz constants, and parameter norms, assuming weight sharing. This line was extended to E(n)-equivariant MPNNs by Karczewski et al. [2024] and refined via PAC-Bayesian analyses by Liao et al. [2021], Ju et al. [2023]; see also Lee et al. [2024] for knowledge graphs. Morris et al. [2023] connected MPNNsвАЩ expressivity and generalization via the VapnikвАУChervonenkis theory, showing that VC dimension depends on the number of 1 -WL equivalence classes, logarithmically on the number of colors, and polynomially on the number of parameters. Their discrete pseudo-metric assumption was extended by Pellizzoni et al. [2024], who studied vertex-individualized MPNNs using covering numbers, though without explicit metric bounds. Related refinements include VC lower bounds for restricted MPNNs [Dani√Ђls and Geerts, 2024], margin-based analyses [Franks et al., 2024, Li et al., 2024, Chuang et al., 2021], and more expressive MPNNs [Franks et al., 2024, Maskey et al., 2025]. Several works analyze generalization under structural assumptions. 4Maskey et al. [2022, 2024], Wang et al. [2025] considered random graph models, while Levie [2023], Rauchwerger et al. [2024], Vasileiou et al. [2024] derived bounds using covering numbers. Transductive generalization was studied via algorithmic stability [Verma and Zhang, 2019] and Rademacher complexity under stochastic block models [Esser et al., 2021, Tang and Liu, 2023]. For semi-supervised vertex classification, Baranwal et al. [2021] analyzed MPNNs on mixtures of Gaussians over stochastic block models. Importantly, the above work analyzed MPNNsвАЩ generalization ability in the classical uniform convergence regime. In contrast, the present work examines the generalization of a single function to larger graphs than those seen during training. Yehudai et al. [2021] derived negative generalization results for larger graphs than those seen in the training set, while Levin et al. [2025] derived necessary conditions of generalization to larger graphs. See Vasileiou et al. [2024] for a survey on generalization analyses of MPNNs and related architectures. 

Empirical work on NAR on graphs A large body of empirical work studies NAR for graphs problems [Velickovic and Blundell, 2021, Cappart et al., 2023]. Early empirical studies demon-strated that neural architectures can imitate algorithmic execution when trained on intermediate computation traces [Velickovic et al., 2020]. MPNNs have emerged as a particularly effective backbone for NAR on graph-structured problems due to their close correspondence with local, iterative graph computations [Cappart et al., 2023]. Empirical results show that MPNNs can learn graph algorithms such as single-source shortest paths, breadth-first search, minimum spanning trees, and dynamic programming [P√°ndy et al., 2022, Velickovic et al., 2020, Yonetani et al., 2021]. Subsequent work investigated architectural refinements and training strategies to improve stability and generalization, e.g., [Gr√ґtschla et al., 2022, Ibarz et al., 2022, Jain et al., 2023, J√Љr√Я et al., 2023, Numeroso et al., 2023, Rodionov and Prokhorenkova, 2025, Xhonneux et al., 2021]. Surveys [Cappart et al., 2023] and benchmark studies [Velickovic et al., 2022] further systematized these empirical findings and highlighted both the potential and limitations of machine-learning enhanced approaches to algorithmic reasoning and combinatorial optimization. 

Theoretical work on algorithmic reasoning on graphs There is a substantial body of work studying the expressive capabilities of message-passing neural networks (MPNNs) and related architectures for representing graph algorithms. For example, Loukas [2020] studied the depth and width requirements of MPNNs for solving problems such as minimum vertex cover, leveraging results from distributed computing. Xu et al. [2019], Morris et al. [2019] showed that MPNNs are inherently limited by the 1-WL in their ability to distinguish non-isomorphic graphs. Qian et al. [2024] demonstrated that MPNNs can express each step of the primalвАУdual interior-point method for solving linear optimization problems, while Yau et al. [2024] investigated their ability to represent approximation algorithms for hard combinatorial problems such as maximum cut and minimum vertex cover; see also Sato et al. [2019]. Dudzik and Velickovic [2022] used category-theoretic tools to establish a connection between MPNNs and dynamic programming. Hertrich and Skutella [2023], Hertrich and Sering [2025] studied the size requirements of recurrent neural networks for solving knapsack and maximum-flow problems. More recently, Rosenbluth and Grohe [2025] devised a general framework for understanding the ability of recurrent MPNNs to simulate algorithms on arbitrarily large instances, and He and Vitercik [2025] showed that MPNNs can simulate classical primalвАУdual approximation schemes. In addition, several works have begun to study the ability of transformer architectures to simulate (graph) algorithms, 5e.g., de Luca and Fountoulakis [2024], de Luca et al. [2025], Merrill and Sabharwal [2025], Sanford et al. [2024,], Yehudai et al. [2025], Zhou et al. [2024]. Overall, these works primarily focus on expressivity, largely ignoring questions related to learning and optimization. Xu et al. [2020] addressed this gap by proposing PAC-style sample-complexity bounds for learning common (graph) algorithms with MPNNs, showing how architectural alignment with the target algorithm can improve sample efficiency. Nerem et al. [2025] demonstrated that MPNNs trained on small datasets, equipped with a regularization term, and optimized to a sufficiently small loss can execute the BellmanвАУFord algorithm on arbitrarily large graphs. However, their analysis crucially relies on a non-differentiable regularization term.

An attributed graph is a pair (G, a G) with a graph G = ( V (G), E (G)) and a (vertex-)attribute function aG : V (G) вЖТ Rd, for d > 0. The attribute or feature of v вИИ V (G) is aG(v). Invariants Let G be a set of graphs, a graph-level invariant (regarding G) is a function 

h : G вЖТ Rd, d > 0 such that h(G) = h(H), for G and H being isomorphic. In addition, for 

k > 0, a k-tuple invariant is a function h : Vk(G) вЖТ Rd, such that h(G, v) = h(H, w) whenever 

(G, v) and (H, w) are isomorphic. For k = 0 , this recovers the notion of a graph invariant. 

2.1. Metric spaces and covering numbers 

Here, we define pseudo-metric spaces, continuity assumptions, covering numbers, and partitions, which play an essential role in the following. 

Metric spaces In the remainder of the paper, вАЬdistancesвАЭ between graphs play an essential role, which we make precise by defining a pseudo-metric (on the set of graphs). Let X be a set equipped with a pseudo-metric d : X √Ч X вЖТ R+, i.e., d is a function satisfying d(x, x ) = 0 

and d(x, y ) = d(y, x ) for x, y вИИ X , and d(x, y ) вЙ§ d(x, z ) + d(z, y ), for x, y, z вИИ X . The latter property is called the triangle inequality. The pair (X , d ) is called a pseudo-metric space . For 

(X , d ) to be a metric space , d additionally needs to satisfy d(x, y ) = 0 вЗТ x = y, for x, y вИИ X .1

Lipschitz continuity on metric spaces Let (X , d X ) and (Y, d Y ) be two pseudo-metric spaces. A function f : X вЖТ Y is called cf -Lipschitz continuous , for cf вИИ R>0, if, for x, x вА≤ вИИ X ,

dY (f (x), f (xвА≤)) вЙ§ cf ¬Ј dX (x, x вА≤).

7Covering numbers Let (X , d ) be a pseudo-metric space. Given an ќµ > 0, an ќµ-cover of X is a subset C вКЖ X such that for all elements x вИИ X there is an element y вИИ C such that d(x, y ) вЙ§ ќµ.Given ќµ > 0 and a pseudo-metric d on the set X , we define the covering number of X ,

N (X , d, ќµ ) := min {m | вИГ an ќµ-cover of X of cardinality m},

i.e., the smallest number m such that there exists a ќµ-cover of cardinality m of the set X with regard to the pseudo-metric d.

2.2. Message-passing graph neural networks

We call such a set of functions a graph-level MPNN class . We call a concrete choice of parameters, e.g., (( ut, at)tвИИ[L], r) of an graph-level MPNN architecture parametrization .82.3. The 1-dimensional WeisfeilerвАУLeman algorithm and variants 

Here, we introduce the 1-dimensional WeisfeilerвАУLeman algorithm and some variants. 

The 1-dimensional WeisfeilerвАУLeman algorithm The 1-dimensional WeisfeilerвАУLeman algo-rithm (1-WL ) or color refinement is a well-studied heuristic for the graph isomorphism problem, originally proposed by Weisfeiler and Leman [1968]. 2 Intuitively, the algorithm determines if two graphs are non-isomorphic by iteratively coloring or labeling vertices. Given an initial coloring or labeling of the vertices of both graphs, e.g., their degree or application-specific information, in each iteration, two vertices with the same label get different labels if the number of identically labeled neighbors is unequal. These labels induce a vertex partition, and the algorithm terminates when, after some iteration, the algorithm does not refine the current partition, i.e., when a stable coloring or stable partition is obtained. Then, if the number of vertices with a specific label differs between the two graphs, we can conclude that the graphs are not isomorphic. It is easy to see that the algorithm cannot distinguish all non-isomorphic graphs [Cai et al., 1992]. However, it is a powerful heuristic that can successfully decide isomorphism for a broad class of graphs [Arvind et al., 2015, Babai and Kucera, 1979]. In the following, we formally describe a variant of the 1-WL that also considers edge weights. Formally, let (G, вДУ G) be a labeled graph and let wG : E(G) вЖТ R be an edge-weight function for 

G. In each iteration, t > 0, the 1-WL computes a vertex coloring C1

The 1.1-dimensional WeisfeilerвАУLeman algorithm The 1.1-dimensional WeisfeilerвАУLeman algorithm ((1 ,1) -WL ) [Rattan and Seppelt, 2023, Qian et al., 2024] can be seen as an extension of the 1-iWL , which tries every possible placement for the unique label [вИЧ] and runs the 1-iWL 

in parallel on the disjoint union of these individualized graphs. Formally, the (1 ,1) -WL does 

not distinguish a pair of graphs (G, H ) if there exists a bijection ѕА : V (G) вЖТ V (H) such that, for v вИИ V (G), running the 1-iWL in вАЬparallelвАЭ on G, with v individualized, and H, with ѕА(v)

individualized, does not distinguish between the two graphs. Observe that we can define 1-tuple or graph-level invariants based on these 1-WL variants. 

2.4. Separation and approximation abilities of MPNNs 

Morris et al. [2019] and Xu et al. [2019] established that the graph-distinguishing power of any MPNN architecture is upper bounded by the 1-WL . Moreover, for MPNNs with sum aggregation, Morris et al. [2019] showed that, on any finite set of graphs, suitable parameter choices yield expressivity matching that of 1-WL ; see Grohe [2021] and Morris et al. [2023] for further discussion. Analogous statements can be lifted to the 1-iWL and (1 ,1) -WL settings. To formalize distinguishability, following Azizian and Lelarge [2021] we express the ability of a function class to distinguish graphs via an induced equivalence relation. Let G be a set of graphs, and let F be a class of functions f : G вЖТ D for some domain D. We define the equivalence relation ѕБG (F) on G by 

(G, H ) вИИ ѕБG (F) вЗРвЗТ f (G) = f (H), for all f вИИ F .

In case (G, H ) вИИ ѕБG (F), we say that F cannot distinguish G and H. If F = {f } is a singleton, we write ѕБ(f ) instead of ѕБ({f }).We extend the definition to k-tuple invariants as follows. For a given k вИИ N and for a class F

of functions f : Vk(G) вЖТ D, for some domain D, define the equivalence relation ѕБVk (G)(F) on 

Vk(G) by 

(G, v, H, w) вИИ ѕБVk (G)(F) вЗРвЗТ f (G, v) = f (H, w) for all f вИИ F

While the knapsack problem is not a graph problem in its standard formulation, it admits a classical reduction to a shortest-path problem on a suitably constructed directed graph; see Section 3.2.1. 12 3. What and how can GNNs learn 

Based on the definition of invariants in Section 2, we can view algorithms as invariant mappings from graphs (or their vertices) to scalars or real-valued vectors. Given a hypothesis class, we can compare its distinguishability with that of an algorithm via their induced equivalence relations, closely related to uniform approximation; see Proposition 1. However, these results do not explain how to choose a function in the hypothesis class that approximates a given algorithm, and, moreover, the notion of approximation is non-uniform, applying only to graphs of fixed size. Consequently, it does not, by itself, imply learnability, particularly for larger instances. In this section, we address these limitations by deriving suitable loss functions and finite datasets; training under these settings yields guarantees on how well MPNNs can learn to approximate algorithms uniformly across graph sizes. We distinguish between learning algorithms that are graph-level invariants, e.g., the cost of the minimum spanning tree, and k-tuple invariants, e.g., the shortest-path distance from a source vertex to all other vertices in a graph. 

3.1. Regularization-induced extrapolation 

Here, we develop a general theoretical framework showing that, with suitable regularization and carefully chosen datasets, a learning algorithm can extrapolate beyond the training range, provided the input features lie in a compact set with an appropriate topology. In particular, our results apply to extrapolation to arbitrarily large domains. We establish this theory in a general learning setting by proving basic learnability properties of Lipschitz functions on compact sets. In Section 3.2, we specialize the analysis to MPNNs with different architectures, yielding our main extrapolation (size generalization) results. We begin by defining the notion of a finite Lipschitz class , i.e., a set of parameterized functions over a bounded domain for which the parameters control the Lipschitz constant. 

Definition 2. Let X be a non-empty set. Given a hypothesis class FќШ := {fќЄ : ќЄ вИИ ќШ} with

A target function f вИЧ is approximable with respect to FќШ with certificate Bf вИЧ if for all ќµ > 0, there exists ќЄ вИИ ќШ such that |fќЄ(x) вИТ f вИЧ(x)| < ќµ , for x вИИ X .Let N вИИ N and FќШ be a finite Lipschitz class, let f вИЧ : X вЖТ R be a target function, and let

Example of finite Lipschitz class and certificates A common example a finite Lipschitz class is given by standard feedforward neural networks of the form x 7 вЖТ WLѕГ(WLвИТ1ѕГ(¬Ј ¬Ј ¬Ј ѕГ(W1x) ¬Ј ¬Ј ¬Ј ))

More generally, certificates for compositions are obtained by multiplying per-layer or operator bounds, and many architectures (including constrained residual or normalized variants) admit similarly computable certificates. Likewise, we assume we have a known upper bound Bf вИЧ вИИ R>0 such that the target f вИЧ is Lipschitz regarding dX with minimal Lipschitz constant at most Bf вИЧ .The following result shows that for a finite Lipschitz class, controlling the Lipschitz certificate of that class, and the Lipschitz continuity of the target class imply that a target function can be learned from finite data; see Figure 1 for an illustration. 

Theorem 3 (Informal) . Let FќШ be a finite Lipschitz class on (X , d X ) with certificates BќЄ,and let f вИЧ be a Lipschitz target that is approximable with certificate Bf вИЧ . Then, defining the regularization term as 

Lreg (fќЄ) = ќЈ ReLU( BќЄ вИТ Bf вИЧ ), for some ќЈ > 0,

it follows that for any ќµ > 0, there exist ќµвА≤(ќµ), r (ќµ) > 0, and a dataset X вКВ X with cardinality 

X = N (X , d X , r (ќµ)) such that if the regularized loss on X is smaller than ќµвА≤(ќµ), then 

sup 

> xвИИX

|fќЄ(x) вИТ f вИЧ(x)| < ќµ. 

On the Bf вИЧ certificate assumption The key assumption in Theorem 3 is not that the true 

Lipschitz constant stays controlled while approximating the target, but that the Lipschitz certificate BќЄ does. That is, we can approximate f вИЧ arbitrarily well by functions fќЄ whose computable bounds BќЄ do not blow up. Since BќЄ is directly computable from the parameters (e.g., via operator-norm or other parameter-norm bounds), it can be used in training even when the true Lipschitz constant MќЄ is intractable. This yields a practical regularizer that controls 

BќЄ.14 The bound for Bf вИЧ is trivial if the target function lies in the hypothesis class, but often 

f вИЧ /вИИ F ќШ and must be approximated. For standard FNNs, obtaining a nontrivial bound on 

Bf вИЧ is difficult. However, fully expressive FNN alternatives have been proposed for adversarial robustness [Anil et al., 2019]; their Lipschitz constants can be bounded via parameter constraints, and the resulting function class is dense in the Lipschitz functions, so they can meet the theoremвАЩs conditions. Similarly, 1-Lipschitz residual networks are dense in the set of scalar 1-Lipschitz functions on any compact domain [Murari et al., 2025], with Lipschitz constants bounded through parameter constraints. A more general theorem in Section B addresses cases where functions cannot be approximated with a bounded certificate, including non-Lipschitz targets. 

Connection to distinguishability If the conclusion of Theorem 3 holds, then a necessary condition is that, whenever FќШ cannot distinguish two inputs, f вИЧ must also assign them the same value. This observation is particularly relevant for hypothesis classes with limited distinguishing power, such as MPNNs. In that case, uniform learnability is restricted to targets 

f вИЧ whose distinguishability does not exceed that of the model class. 

3.2. Finite Lipschitzness in MPNNs 

Below, we study MPNNs that satisfy the finite Lipschitz learning property of Definition 2, which suffices for the learnability guarantee in Theorem 3. Throughout, the input space consists of pairs (G, u ) with a graph G and vertex u вИИ V (G), i.e., V1(G) for a graph class G.We equip V1(G) with a pseudo-metric that controls the Lipschitz behavior of MPNNs. Our analysis covers (i) normalized sum aggregation, (ii) mean aggregation, and (iii) max/min aggregation; see Section G for formal definitions. These are special cases of the general MPNN formulation in Equation (1) and capture many classical graph algorithms. We first establish finite Lipschitzness for normalized sum aggregation and extend it to mean aggregation. While inspired by work on iterated degree measures and computation trees (e.g., Greb√≠k and Rocha [2022], B√ґker et al. [2023], Rauchwerger et al. [2024]), our approach does not require compactness of the input space, i.e., Definition 2 only assumes finite covering numbers, simplifying the construction (see Section I). Crucially, we also show that max/min aggregationвАФessential for many algorithmic invariantsвАФ satisfies finite Lipschitzness. Here, the analysis proceeds on Hausdorff spaces with the Hausdorff distance; see Section J. To our knowledge, these are the first explicit Lipschitzness guarantees for max- and min-aggregation MPNNs, despite widespread use of related stability arguments [Levie, 2023, B√ґker et al., 2023, Rauchwerger et al., 2024, Vasileiou et al., 2024]. These architectures cover a range of common graph algorithms (see Section 3.2.1). Precise assumptions, conditions, and constants appear in Section G; we state the main result informally next. 

Theorem 4 (Informal) . The hypothesis class FќШ induced by MPNNs using normalized sum aggregation, mean aggregation, or max (or min) aggregation satisfies Definition 2. 

See Theorem 104 and Theorem 108 for the formal statements and proofs. In the following, we identify graph invariants expressible by these MPNN architectures, and hence learnable from finite data by Theorem 4. 15 3.2.1. Algorithms within a finite Lipschitz class 

Here, we outline examples of algorithms that are contained in finite Lipschitz classes, follow-ing Definition 2, there exists a (pseudo-)metric with finite covering number, and the algorithm is Lipschitz with respect to this (pseudo-)metric. 

Normalized-sum aggregation In particular, normalized-sum aggregation is of special interest, as on graphs with a fixed number of vertices it allows MPNNs to represent any graph invariant with distinguishing power equivalent to the 1-WL . However, as we will see in Section 4.2, this learnability property cannot be maintained without restricting attention to graphs of fixed order.

Therefore, by Theorem 3, a finite training set with regularization suffices to train an MPNN to learn truncated PageRank and extrapolate to graphs of arbitrary size. Moreover, since truncation error is size-independent, the trained model approximates full PageRank to the same error across all graphs, despite being trained on finite data.

By Theorem 4, MPNNs with min aggregation satisfy Definition 2, and since the number of iterations K needed to compute shortest paths is at most the graph diameter, which is independent of graph size for bounded-diameter graph families, it follows from Theorem 3 that a finite training set with regularization can be used to train an MPNN to learn BellmanвАУFord, enabling learning from a finite number of training examples that will extrapolate to graphs of arbitrary size. In Section 3.3, we adopt an approach specifically designed for this setting to derive a small training set that guarantees extrapolation. More generally, Zhu et al. [2021] showed that a generalized BellmanвАУFord algorithm, based on path formulations, captures invariants such as the Katz index, widest path, and most reliable path. These can likewise be represented by MPNNs with mean and max aggregation and thus fall into a finite Lipschitz hypothesis class. 

Dynamic programming It is well-known that many problems that can be solved by dynamic programming can be cast as a shortest-path problem on a transformed graph [Frieze, 1976]. Using the 0-1 knapsack problem as an illustration, for n вИИ N, given items i вИИ [n] with integer weights si and values vi, and a capacity S, consider the directed acyclic graph with vertices 

(i, j ), for i вИИ [n]0 and j вИИ [S]0, where (i, j ) represents having considered the first i items and accumulated total weight j. Now, we add edges (( i вИТ 1, j ), (i, j )) of weight 0 (вАЬdo not choose item iвАЭ) and, whenever j + si вЙ§ S, edges (( i вИТ 1, j ), (i, j + si)) of weight вИТvi (вАЬchoose item iвАЭ); finally, connect each vertex (n, j ) to a sink t by a zero-weight edge. Then every s-t path (with

We note that the same reasoning applies to a large class of problems that can be cast as dynamic programs, e.g., the longest increasing subsequence or edit distances between strings. 

3.3. Improved learning guarantees for SSSPs algorithms 

While the above results demonstrate the existence of a finite training set for learning various algorithms, they do not explicitly show how to construct this training set or bound its size. Hence, the following result shows that we can explicitly construct a constant-size training set and a differentiable regularization term such that a small loss on the training set implies approximating the BellmanвАУFord algorithm for arbitrarily large graphs. Consider learning K-steps of BellmanвАУFord using a min-aggregation MPNN with K layers and m-layer feed-forward neural networks. We train the MPNN by minimizing a loss function L(ќЄ) that contains a 17 weighted variant of вДУ1-regularization. In particular, Lreg (ќЄ) is a weighted sum over the вДУ1 norms of the weight matrices and bias vectors, with layer-dependent weights. 

Theorem 5 (Informal) . There exists a training set X of size K + 1 such that, for appropriate choice of regularization parameter ќЈ, if a K-layer min-aggregation MPNN achieves a loss LX (ќЄ)

within ќµ < 1/2 of its global minimum, then for any SSSP instance, the MPNN approximates every K-step shortest path distance x(K) within additive error ќµ(x(K) + 1) .

A detailed statement of the theorem is given in Theorem 17 in the appendix. The theorem differs from the main theorem of Nerem et al. [2025] in several ways. On the positive side, our result uses a smaller training set, namely K + 1 path instances, and replaces their non-differentiable вДУ0 penalty with a differentiable вДУ1 regularizer with layer-specific weights. This differentiability allows our loss to be optimized directly. On the other hand, our analysis assumes a somewhat more restricted model, i.e, the depth is fixed to exactly K message-passing layers (matching K BellmanвАУFord steps), and the aggregation dimension is assumed to be 1.These choices are made to simplify the analysis. Furthermore, in our result, the regularization parameter ќЈ and the edge weights in the training set both scale exponentially in K, which may be prohibitive in some settings. However, in Section D.2 we outline ways to circumvent these limitations. 

# 4. What GNNs cannot learn 

In the previous section, we identified sufficient conditions under which a hypothesis class admits learnability guarantees via regularized empirical risk minimization. In particular, when a class of MPNNs forms a finite Lipschitz class with respect to a suitable (pseudo)metric on the input space, invariant algorithms that can be uniformly approximated within this class are learnable from finite samples. In this section, we turn to the complementary question, i.e., which invariant algorithms cannot be learned by MPNN hypothesis classes? We first show cases of invariants that are not expressible by any MPNN of the general form Equation (1). Then we show that even for invariants that are expressible by MPNNs, Theorem 3 may not be applied since Definition 2 is not satisfied by these types of MPNNs. 

4.1. Expressivity limitations 

Here, we show that MPNNs are often insufficient to learn simple graph algorithms. To that end, we first show that standard MPNNs, as defined in Equation (1), are not expressive enough to determine the costs of the SSSP and MST. Formally, let G denote the class of edge-weighted graphs. Given an edge-weighted graph 

(G, w G) and a source vertex s вИИ V (G), we view the costs for the SSSP problem as a 2-tuple invariant SSSP : V2(G) вЖТ R such that SSSP (G, (s, v )) := cost G(PG(s, v )) , for v вИИ V (G), where 

PG(s, v ) denotes a shortest path from s to v in G. Similarly, we view the cost of the MST problem as a graph-level invariant MST : G вЖТ R such that MST (G) := вИС  

> eвИИE(T)

wG(e), for a minimal spanning tree T of G.The following results highlight limitations of MPNN architectures in approximating classical graph algorithmic invariants. In particular, no MPNN architecture can approximate the SSSP cost or the MST cost arbitrarily well. Moreover, this limitation persists for MST even when considering 1 -iWL -simulating MPNNs; see Section 2.4. Formal statements and proofs are deferred to Section E. 18 Proposition 6 (Informal) . There does not exist an MPNN architecture that can approximate the invariants SSSP and MST . In contrast, there exist 1 -iWL - and (1,1) -WL -expressive MPNN architectures that can approximate the invariants SSSP and MST , respectively. 

Here, 1 -iWL and (1,1) -WL refer to MPNN architectures that simulate individualized variants of the 1-WL , as introduced in Section 2.3. 

4.2. Expressible but (possibly) not learnable invariants 

Beyond expressivity limitations, there exist invariant algorithms that are representable by MPNNs but for which our sufficient conditions for learnability do not apply. In such cases, the obstruction is not a lack of expressive power, but rather the absence of a metric structure on the input space that yields finite covering numbers, as required by Definition 2. As a simple example, consider a variant of the MPNN architecture in which the aggregation operator in Equation (1) is replaced by an unnormalized sum. Restricting to graphs without vertex or edge features (or, equivalently, to graphs with constant vertex and edge features across all vertices and edges) and to one-layer architectures, the resulting hypothesis class clearly contains the degree invariant (G, u ) 7 вЖТ deg G(u), since vertex degrees are computed exactly by summing over neighbors. However, if we donвАЩt restrict our space to graphs with bounded maximum degree, this expressivity already prevents the hypothesis class from being a finite Lipschitz class. Indeed, for any (pseudo-)metric under which the degree map is Lipschitz with a finite constant, the induced metric space necessarily has infinite covering number (for sufficiently small radius) as shown next. 

Lemma 7 (Informal) . Let K be the family of all complete graphs. Let d be any (pseudo-)metric on V1(K) such that the degree invariant 

deg : V1(K) вЖТ N

is L-Lipschitz for some L вИИ R>0. Then, for every ќµ вИИ (0 , 1 

> L

), N (V1(K), d, ќµ ) = вИЮ. Consequently, no hypothesis class containing the degree invariant can satisfy Definition 2 on any graph space containing V1(K).

A similar obstruction applies to any invariant equivalent to the 1-WL , since its first iteration already captures the degree invariant. Thus, any hypothesis class expressive enough to repre-sent all 1-WL invariants inherits the same covering-number pathology on graph spaces with unbounded degrees. 

# 5. Limitations and future directions 

While our framework provides the first general, provable learning guarantees for a broad class of graph algorithms with MPNNs, it relies on several structural assumptions that limit its scope. In particular, our theory assumes access to carefully constructed training datasets that form suitable covers of the underlying graph space. Although such datasets are essential for establishing our guarantees, they may not arise naturally in practice, so it is necessary to develop high-probability, sampling-based methods for constructing informative training sets. Moreover, our results are formulated in terms of achieving sufficiently small regularized training 19 Table 1: Test score results for Q2 with a standard and 1-iWL -equivalent MPNNs. All results were obtained across three seeds, and test scores are averaged. The number in brackets indicates the number of vertices for the test graphs.                                                                  

> Dataset Task (Test score вЖУ)ER-constdeg ER General
> 1-WL (64) 0.9725 ¬±0.0001 0.9725 ¬±0.0001 0.8393 ¬±0.0001 1-iWL (64) 0.0035 ¬±0.0002 0.0034 ¬±0.0004 0.0032 ¬±0.0002 1-WL (256) 0.9896 ¬±0.0006 0.9765 ¬±0.0001 0.8368 ¬±0.0010 1-iWL (256) 0.0033 ¬±0.0002 0.0037 ¬±0.0001 0.0033 ¬±0.0002 1-WL (1024) 0.9948 ¬±0.0005 0.9645 ¬±0.0001 0.8217 ¬±0.0010 1-iWL (1024) 0.0030 ¬±0.0001 0.0038 ¬±0.0002 0.0033 ¬±0.0002

loss. Yet, we do not provide guarantees that standard gradient-based optimization methods will reliably converge to parameter settings that generalize. Hence, looking forward , bridging our learning-theoretic analysis with convergence results for gradient descent, therefore, remains an important direction for future work. Finally, our current analysis is restricted to polynomial-time algorithms; extending the framework to approximation algorithms for computationally hard problems and studying whether exploiting the data distribution can yield approximation ratios beyond worst-case guarantees constitutes another promising avenue for future research. 

# 6. Experimental study 

In the following, we investigate to what extent our theoretical results translate into practice. Specifically, we answer the following questions. 

Q1 Does gradient descent converge to parameter assignments that allow for size generalization? 

Q2 Do the more expressive MPNN architectures of Section 4 lead to improved predictive performance in practice? 

Q3 Does the differentiable regularization term from Section 3.3 leads to improved generalization errors compared to p-norm based regularization term? We use an MPNN aligned with the theoretical results observed in Section 3.3. Due to the explicit construction of the training set, we conduct the experiments on the SSSP problem outlined in Section 3.3. For this, we generate synthetic training and test datasets based on Erd≈СsвАУR√©nyi graphs and path graphs derived from Theorem 5. We then train a two-layer MPNN to predict two steps of the BellmanвАУFord algorithm. See Section L for details on dataset construction, experimental settings, and additional results. The source code of all methods and evaluation procedures is available at https://github.com/Timo-SH/exact_nar .

Results and discussion In the following, we address Q1 to Q3 . Regarding Q1 , Section 6 shows size generalization properties for 64 and 1024 vertices on test graphs. The underlying MPNNs were trained on the same training dataset with a fixed edge weight for the path graphs outlined in Section 3.3. In addition, weights were uniformly sampled for test set edges. To demonstrate size generalization, we provide three test sets with increasing graph diversity. Throughout all test sets, the test error does not increase with vertex count in each graph. Regarding Q2 , Table 1 shows the inability of a standard MPNN to generalize to unseen graphs in the test set at all. In addition, compared to the 1-iWL -equivalent MPNN, we observe a significantly higher training loss. Regarding Q3 , as seen in Table 2, replacing the proposed differentiable regularization term with a p-norm based regularization leads to similar results using вДУ1 regularization. While вДУ1 and 

вДУ2 norms allow for sufficient training and generalization results, size generalization improves with the proposed regularization term across graph sizes. Furthermore, with вДУ2 regularization, training is noticeably less stable and yields slightly worse test set performance. These results align with Theorem 5, highlighting the application of a differentiable regularization term to the SSSP problem, opposed to a non-differentiable regularization previously required. 21 7. Conclusion 

We developed a general theoretical framework for learning graph algorithms with GNNs. Our framework characterizes the conditions under which an MPNN, or more expressive variants, can be trained on a finite set of instances and provably generalize to inputs of arbitrary size by minimizing a supervised, regularized loss. By connecting algorithmic learning to notions of metric structure, covering numbers, and regularization-induced extrapolation, we move beyond purely expressivity-based analyses and provide learning-theoretic guarantees for neural algorithmic reasoning on graphs. Building on this framework, we identified broad classes of graph algorithms, ranging from shortest paths to dynamic programming problems, that suitably expressive GNNs can learn, and we also identified fundamental limitations of standard MPNNs. For the single-source shortest-path problem, we further showed how a differentiable p-norm-based regularization significantly reduces the size of the required training set. In summary, our results provide a precise characterization of which algorithms GNNs can learn from finite data and which they cannot, thereby enabling a more principled understanding of data-driven algorithmic design and its potential for reliable generalization beyond the training regime.

# B. Learning non-realizable functions 

This appendix records a variant of Theorem 3 that applies to a larger class of target functions, particularly functions that cannot be approximated with a bounded Lipschitz constant. The approximation properties of the target are summarized by a certificate profile , which captures how large a certificate budget is required to approximate the target to a given accuracy.

Remark 11 . The dependence on ќЈ and the tolerance ќµвА≤ appears through the term ќµвА≤/ќЈ in (5) :weaker regularization (smaller ќЈ) permits larger certificates BќЄ вЙ§ ЋЬBf вИЧ (ќµ/ 4) + ќµвА≤/ќЈ , which shrinks the admissible cover radius and increases the sample size N (X , d X , r ). Conversely, stronger regularization tightens certificate control, but may increase the achievable regularized loss when an accurate approximation requires larger budgets (as captured by ЋЬBf вИЧ (¬Ј)). 33 C. Improved learning guarantees for SSSPs algorithms 

While the above sections derive conditions under which a finite training dataset exists, and the algorithm can be learned, here we derive a concrete, small training dataset for learning the BellmanвАУFord algorithm for the SSSP problem. Our analysis significantly extends the analysis of Nerem et al. [82] by deriving a simpler training dataset and, unlike the former work, a differentiable regularization term. In the following, we formalize the BellmanвАУFord update, which corresponds exactly to the update performed by the BellmanвАУFord algorithm at each iteration on all vertices of a graph. Our objective is to learn the K-fold application of this update. 

Definition 12 (BellmanвАУFord instance, update, and distance) . An attributed, edge-weighted graph G is called a BellmanвАУFord instance (BF instance) if both its vertex labels aG(v) вЙ• 0,for all v вИИ V (G), and its edge weights wG(e) вЙ• 0, for all e вИИ E(G), are non-negative real-valued functions. For notational convenience, we additionally assume that 

NG(v) := NG(v) вИ™ { v}, wG(v, v ) = 0 , for all v вИИ V (G).

We denote by GBF the set of all BF instances. We further define the map ќУ : GBF вЖТ G BF to be the operator that maps a BF instance G to the BF instance ќУ (G) with the same vertices, edges, and edge weights as G, but whose vertex labels are updated according to 

aќУ (G)(v) := min {aG(u) + wG(u, v ) : u вИИ NG(v)}, for v вИИ V (G).

We refer to ќУ as the BellmanвАУFord update . Note that ќУ (G) вИИ G BF , for G вИИ G BF .Given G вИИ G BF , we define the t-step BellmanвАУFord distance of a vertex v as 

x(t) 

> v

:= aќУ t(G)(v),

where ќУ t denotes the t-fold composition of ќУ .Consequently, the learning task studied in this work is to approximate the vertex-level mapping induced by ќУ K using an MPNN.

[82] in several ways. On the positive side, our result uses a smaller training set, namely K + 1 path instances from TSx,K ,and replaces their non-differentiable вДУ0 penalty with a differentiable вДУ1 regularizer that has layer-specific weights w(k). This differentiability allows our loss to be optimized directly. On the other hand, our analysis assumes a more restricted model: the depth is fixed to exactly 

K message-passing layers (matching K BellmanвАУFord steps), and the aggregation dimension is 1. These choices are made to simplify the analysis. Furthermore, unlike Nerem et al. [82] ,the regularization parameter ќЈ and the training weight scale x both scale exponentially in K,which may be prohibitive in some settings. 

# D. Proof of Theorem 17 

We begin with a proof outline that highlights the main lemmas and ideas underlying the argument. 38 D.1. Proof outline 

Here, we outline the high-level idea of the proof, broken down into its key aspects. 

Section D.3: Properties of FNNs The aim of this section is to develop algebraic properties of FNNs that are needed to handle the ReLU nonlinearity and to investigate the dependence of the network output on its weights and biases. Later, we will see that the features produced by the MPNN can be upper-bounded by the output of a single FNN of depth J (the total number of global layers of the MPNN), which is where the results derived in this section will be applied. The main results include linearity of FNNs in the regime where all parameters are non-negative, as well as monotonicity with respect to both the network parameters and the input. The key ingredient is that, for non-negative inputs, the ReLU activation acts as the identity. In addition, we prove the following corollary. 

Corollary 18. Let W вИИ ќШW and B, BC вИИ ќШb. Then for any y вИИ Rd0 

> вЙ•0

it holds 

> вИ£вИ£

FNN (J)(W, B + BC )( y) вИТ FNN (J)(W+, B+ 

> C

)( y)вИ£вИ£ вЙ§ G(W, BC )( y) + FNN (J)(|W| , |B| )(0) .

The RHS consists of two terms, each capturing a distinct source of deviation. The second term, FNN (J)(|W| , |B| )(0) , can be interpreted as the maximal influence that non-zero biases may exert on the network output. In particular, once we establish that the biases are small, this term becomes negligible. The first term, G(W, BC )( y), is defined as a sum of auxiliary FNNs and quantifies the maximal contribution of negative weights. After proving that all negative weights must be close to zero near optimality, this term is likewise small. Consequently, the entire RHS provides a controlled error term. Interpreting the LHS in the MPNN setting, B represents the collection of biases of the MPNN, while BC has the same structure and encodes the contribution of edge features along a path to a node v in the training set. Thus, the LHS measures the difference between the output of the MPNN with parameters (W, C, B) (cf. Section C.1) and that of the MPNN with parameters 

(W+, C+, 0), i.e., where all weights are replaced by their positive parts and all biases are set to zero. The latter parameter configuration is significantly easier to analyze, since the effects of the ReLU nonlinearity and the biases vanish. Hence, this corollary allows us to replace the original MPNN features by those of the simplified network up to an error controlled by the RHS. 

Section D.4: FNNs along computation trees In this section, we define the set of computation trees T KG (v) that encodes all possible choices during aggregation. We then define the FNN along a computation tree t вИИ T KG (v), denoted by H(k)(ќЄ)( t). Intuitively, this quantity represents the feature that the MPNN would output if the aggregation followed the computation tree t.This framework allows us to define the set of minimum-aggregation trees T kG, ќЄ(v), i.e., the computation trees selected by the min-aggregation. In particular, the feature of the MPNN can be written as an FNN evaluated along a minimum-aggregation tree. 

Lemma 19. Let G вИИ G BF , v вИИ V (G), and ќЄ вИИ ќШBF . Then for any k вИИ [K]0 and any 

ѕД kv вИИ T kG, ќЄ(v),

h(k) 

> v

= H(k)(ќЄ)( ѕД kv ).

39 The main purpose of introducing FNNs along computation trees is to enable comparisons between the MPNN output and the output obtained from alternative computation trees. In particular, this allows us to upper-bound the MPNN features by FNNs evaluated along trees that are not chosen by the MPNN itself. This idea is formalized in the next lemma, which shows that although the MPNN performs a layer-wise, вАЬgreedyвАЭ minimization, the resulting computation tree ѕД kv вИИ T KG, ќЄ(v) minimizes the feature globally, up to the effect of negative weights. 

Lemma 20. Let G вИИ G BF , v вИИ V (G), and ќЄ вИИ ќШBF . Then for any k вИИ [K]0, ѕД kv вИИ T kG, ќЄ(v),and tkv вИИ T kG (v) it holds

element-wise. 

This is the point where the use of min-aggregation becomes crucial.

> вЙ•0

, which can be represented as an FNN with suitably chosen biases. As a consequence, it inherits all properties derived in Section D.3. In particular, Corollary 18 translates directly into the following corollary for walk-lifted FNNs. 

Corollary 21. For all z вИИ RK+1  

> вЙ•0

and parameters (W, C, B) вИИ ќШBF , it holds

Here, B(z, C) represents the effective bias induced by the edge features encoded in z and the parameters C. As before, the right-hand side compares the walk-lifted FNN with arbitrary parameters to the corresponding object with non-negative weights and vanishing biases. We further show that the walk-lifted FNN coincides with the FNN along a computation tree whenever the computation tree is essentially a walk.

Here, tK (p) denotes the computation tree associated with p. The condition tK (p) вИИ T KG, ќЄ(v)

means that the successive minimum-aggregations in the MPNN select exactly the nodes along the path p, that is, in each layer the aggregation chooses the next vertex on p. In the subsequent sections, this lemma will serve as the key ingredient for replacing MPNN features with an object that is significantly easier to control and analyze. 40 Section D.6: Small empirical loss and structure of parameters The goal of this section is to show that if the loss is close to its global minimum and if the parameters ќЈ and x are chosen sufficiently large, then the network parameters must exhibit a highly constrained structure. More precisely, we prove that

The estimate (12) implies that the effect of the biases on the MPNN output is negligible, i.e. 

B вЙИ 0. The bound (13) shows that the factors multiplying the edge weights are close to one (or larger). Inequality (14) allows us to replace MPNN features along a path by the simpler expression Hwl (W+, C+, 0)( z), and the final inequality ensures that the empirical loss is small. The key idea in the proof is that the previous lemma allows us to analyze the modified loss 

ЋЬL instead of L. The modified loss removes couplings between the factors ќ≥k and eliminates ReLU nonlinearities between layers, thereby isolating the two competing forces acting on the parameters. The regularization drives B, the negative weights wвИТ, and the factors ќ≥k, k вИИ [K]0,toward zero, while the empirical loss pushes B and the ќ≥kвАЩs away from zero. This implies 

wвИТ вЙИ 0. Since exp (L) < ќЈ by assumption, the regularization force on B dominates, yielding 

B вЙИ 0. Finally, for sufficiently large x, the loss is minimized when ќ≥k вЙИ 1, which implies that the regularization loss is approximately L and hence that the empirical loss is small. 

Section D.7: Upper and lower nound In this section, we additionally assume that the hidden dimension before each minimum aggregation is equal to 1. Intuitively, this restriction ensures that the minimum aggregation cannot decrease hidden features on graphs that contain more branching than simple path graphs, which provide strictly fewer aggregation options. As a result, path graphs represent the worst case for the minimum operator. Formally, this assumption implies that the MPNN features can be expressed via a walk-lifted FNN along any computation path. This allows us to apply the parameter characterization from Lemma 27 and deduce the following lower bound. 43 Corollary 28 (Lower bound) . Let ќЄ вИИ ќШBF , ќЈ вЙ• 2K exp (L), and x вЙ• 2N ќЈJ . Assume

The first inequality shows that the MPNN features can be bounded from below by ap-proximately the length of any min-aggregation computation path. Since, by definition, the BellmanвАУFord distance x(K) 

> v

is the minimal length among all paths ending at v вИИ V (G), this immediately implies the claimed lower bound. We next establish the corresponding upper bound. Here, an additional difficulty arises, i.e., although the modified loss is minimized at ќ≥k = 1 , it does not provide a tight a priori upper bound on ќ≥k, since the additional regularization cost incurred by choosing ќ≥k > 1 is relatively small. We therefore first show that excessively large values of ќ≥k necessarily lead to overly large MPNN features and hence to an empirical loss that is too large. This observation allows us to control ќ≥k from above and, in combination with Lemma 23, to derive the desired upper bound. 

Theorem 29 (Upper bound) . Let ќЄ вИИ ќШBF , ќЈ вЙ• 2K exp (L), and x вЙ• 2N ќЈJ . Assume

D.2. Outlook 

In this subsection, we outline several directions in which the results of this paper can be strengthened and extended. 

Removing the exponential dependence on ќЈ without biases We first observe that if all biases are set to zero, the proofs reveal that the exponential lower bound on the regularization parameter ќЈ is no longer necessary. In this setting, the arguments can be repeated with only minor modifications, yielding the following result. 

Theorem 30. Let K вИИ N and suppose we aim to learn K steps of the BellmanвАУFord algorithm, i.e. the function ќУ K , using a min-aggregation MPNN with K layers and m-layer feedforward neural networks as update and aggregation functions, as described in Section C.1, but without any biases. Assume that the BellmanвАУFord path training set is contained in the training set, i.e. TSx,K вКВ X, and that the edge-weight scaling satisfies x вЙ• 4mKN ќЈ , where N := |X| is the cardinality of the training set. Then there exists a constant C > 0 such that if L(ќЄ) lies within 0 вЙ§ ќµ < min {ќЈ2mK (K +3) , 12 }

of its global minimum, then for any BellmanвАУFord instance G вИИ G BF and any v вИИ V (G),

Figure 4: Edge case graph from Definition 31 for learning the K-fold BellmanвАУFord update with higher aggregation dimension. The root vertex is vвИТ1. Shown here is the instance for 

K = 4 . At each vertex vi, i вИИ [K], the network can choose between multiple paths of equal total weight x.

Removing the exponential dependence with biases The exponential lower bound on ќЈ can also be removed without eliminating biases by including the BellmanвАУFord path training set twice with different edge-weight scalings, for example, by assuming that TSx,K , T S2x,K вКВ X. By comparing feature values obtained from identical path graphs with different scalings, we can eliminate the bias terms in a first step and show that Lemp (ќЄ) вЙ§ 2ќµ and B вЙ§ ќµ/ќЈ without any restriction on ќЈ. In a second step, bounds analogous to those in Lemma 27 can be derived. The proofs of the upper and lower bounds then remain unchanged. 

Extension to вДУ1-regularization We further aim to generalize our analysis to standard вДУ1-regularization. From a theoretical perspective, we conjecture that size generalization results as in Theorem 17 continue to hold in this setting, though potentially with weaker error bounds and increased optimization difficulty. 

Removing the one-dimensional aggregation assumption Finally, we seek to remove the assumption that the hidden dimension before each aggregation equals one, i.e. to extend the theory to dak > 1. This assumption is only used in Section D.7. For higher aggregation dimensions, the minimum operator may exploit additional degrees of freedom and produce feature values that are significantly smaller than the true shortest-path distance, thereby breaking generalization. We conjecture that this issue can be resolved by augmenting the training set with a carefully designed edge case graph that forces the MPNN to confront the most extreme configurations of shortest paths. 

Definition 31 (Edge case graph) . Let x > 0 and K вИИ N. We define the edge case graph 

G(x, K ) = ( V, E ) as follows. 45 First, add a path with vertices vвИТ1, v 0, . . . , v K вИИ V and edges (viвИТ1, v i) вИИ E for i вИИ [K]0, with edge weights w(vвИТ1, v 0) = x and w(viвИТ1, v i) = 0 for all i вИИ [K].Next, for each i вИИ [K], add a path of length i + 1 from vвИТ1 to vi whose edge weights are zero except for the final edge, which has weight x. More precisely, for each i вИИ [K] we introduce vertices pi,j вИИ V for j вИИ [i вИТ 1] 0 and edges 

{vвИТ1, p i, 0}, {pi, 0, p i, 1}, . . . , {pi,i вИТ2, p i,i вИТ1}, {pi,i вИТ1, v i} вИИ E, 

with weights 

w({vвИТ1, p i, 0}) = w({pi, 0, p i, 1}) = ¬Ј ¬Ј ¬Ј = w({pi,i вИТ2, p i,i вИТ1}) = 0 , w({pi,i вИТ1, v i}) = x. 

Finally, we assign vertex features corresponding to the one-step BellmanвАУFord distances with root vertex vвИТ1. Specifically, we set a(vвИТ1) = a(pi, 0) = 0 for all i вИИ [K], a(v0) = x, and a(v) = ќ≤

for all remaining vertices v вИИ V \ { vвИТ1, p 1,0, . . . , p K, 0, v 0}, where ќ≤ > 0 is chosen sufficiently large. We conjecture that augmenting the training set with the edge case graph G(x, K ) suffices to restore size generalization guarantees with a constant aggregation dimension, and that, in this regime, the performance gap between The tailored loss used in this work and the standard 

вДУ1-loss vanish.

D.4. FNNs along computation trees 

To avoid analyzing the layers of the MPNN one by one, we aim to express the entire MPNN in terms of an object that can be defined similarly to the FNN in the previous section. Unfortunately, in general, no choice of bias and weight vectors in FNN (J)(W, B)( y) can capture the full expressivity of the aggregation operation. To address this, we introduce FNNs along computation paths in this section. For an appropriate choice of the underlying tree, these networks coincide with the MPNN output. Recall that each layer of the MPNN is given by

D.5. Application to walk-lifted FNNs 

In this section, we introduce a third and final parametrized FNN, the walk-lifted FNN, to easily access all relevant parameters. We will see that the walk-lifted FNN inherits all of the properties of the simple FNN as shown in Section D.3. Moreover, we show that when the aggregation indeed follows a single path, the MPNN can be expressed by the walk-lifted FNN.

Remark 52 (1D aggregation) . If dak = 1 for all k вИИ [K]. then any tree in T KG (v) is in fact a walk, and we can identify 

T KG (v) вЙ° P KG (v).

In this case we write zt for t вИИ T KG (v), meaning zp where p вИИ P KG (v) is such that tK (p) = t.Next, we define the walk-lifted FNN, which is a simple FNN as in Section D.3, but with a very specifically chosen bias vector. Intuitively, we choose the bias vector such that each edge weight along a given walk is added in the corresponding edge-insertion layer. In general, the walk-lifted FNN does not capture the same expressivity as a FNN along a tree, and thus is not powerful enough to express any possible feature of the MPNN. In case any minimum aggregation tree is essentially a walk, we will see that the walk-lifted FNN is in fact equal to the feature of the MPNN.

In the preceding sections, we introduced and analyzed three objects: FNNs, FNNs along computation trees, and walk-lifted FNNs. We first introduced FNNs as a simple setting in which we could derive fundamental structural properties. These properties were then applied in the subsequent sections to the aggregation and update functions of the MPNN, as well as to walk-lifted FNNs. Next, FNNs along computation trees enabled us to express MPNN features in closed form, allowing us to compare the MPNN output with that obtained when aggregation follows a computation tree other than the min-aggregation tree. The walk-lifted FNN is the main object we will work with from now on. As shown above, it inherits the relevant properties of simple FNNs while explicitly revealing its dependence on the network parameters and the edge weights along a given path. This explicit structure makes it particularly convenient to analyze. Moreover, as established in the previous lemma, the walk-lifted FNN can be used to express or bound the features of the MPNN. We will use these results as follows. In the next section, we derive bounds on the network parameters under the assumption that the loss is close to its global minimum. To this end, we apply the previous lemma to obtain a lower bound on the empirical loss by removing the ReLU nonlinearity and replacing the MPNN feature hKv with the walk-lifted FNN Hwl (ќЄ+)( zp).Finally, in the last section, we show that for the path training samples TSx,K , the min-aggregation computation tree must follow the path and is therefore essentially a walk. In this case as well, the MPNN feature can be replaced by the walk-lifted FNN, allowing us to work entirely within this framework. 56 D.6. Small empirical loss and structure of parameters 

For the remainder of this section, we will consider the MPNN and its parameters as defined in Section C.

Based on the previous lemma, which allows us to analyze the modified loss ЋЬL in place of the original loss L, the next result identifies the key structural properties that any parameter set must satisfy when it lies close to the global minimum. There are essentially two main advantages to working with ЋЬL.First, the feature contribution is replaced by a simpler upper bound that no longer depends on the ReLUвАЩs intricate behaviour. An immediate consequence is that non-zero biases or negative entries in the weight matrices can only increase the modified loss, rather than interact in more complicated ways as in the original formulation. Secondly, the parameters appearing 62 in different summands of the modified empirical loss become independent of one another. In particular, increasing ќ≥k1 for some k1 does not decrease the term ѕГ((1 вИТ ќ≥k2 )x вИТ exp (L)B)

associated with any k2ћЄ = k1. These simplifications eliminate several sources of coupling and nonlinearity present in the original loss and thereby make the subsequent analysis considerably more tractable. 

Lemma 70 (Parameter characterization near the global minimum) . Let ќЄ вИИ ќШBF . Assume 

TSx,K вКВ X and that L(ќЄ) lies within 0 вЙ§ ќµ вЙ§ ќЈL of its global minimum. Further assume 

ќЈ вЙ• 2K exp( L) and x вЙ• 2N ќЈJ.

Hwl (|W| , |C| , |B| )(0) вЙ§ ќµ, (16) 

ќ≥k вЙ• 1 вИТ ќµќЈJ , k вИИ [K]0, (17)

Moreover, 

Lemp (ќЄ) вЙ§ 2ќµ. 

Note that both ќЈ and x must be chosen sufficiently large. A large value of ќЈ ensures that the influence of the biases on the empirical loss is small compared to their contribution to the regularization term, so that any non-zero bias is strongly penalized overall. Likewise, taking 

x much larger than ќЈ guarantees that the empirical loss dominates the regularizer, so that deviations in the feature values have a significantly stronger impact on the total loss than variations in the regularization cost.

# E. What MPNN cannot learn 

This appendix provides additional details for Section 4. In particular, Section E.1 contains further details related to Section 4.1, including a formal statement and proof of Proposition 6, while Section E.2 contains the proof of Lemma 7 from Section 4.2. 66 E.1. Expressivity limitations 

We begin by formally stating and proving the negative result of Proposition 6, namely that standard MPNN architectures cannot approximate the SSSP and MST invariants.

Proof. For (1) it suffices to consider the edge-weighted graph (G, w G) with V (G) := [6] and edge set E(G) := {(1 ,2) , (1 ,3) , (2 ,3) , (3 ,4) , (4 ,5) , (4 ,6) , (5 ,6) }, with edge weights wG(1 ,2) := 3 ,

wG(1 ,3) := 1 , wG(2 ,3) := 1 , wG(3 ,4) := 5 , wG(4 ,5) := 1 , wG(4 ,6) := 1 , and wG(5 ,6) := 3 .Hence, the graph G consists of two triangles connected by an edge with a large weight. Let s = 1 , t1 = 2 , and t2 = 5 . Then, the shortest path from s to t1 has cost 2 via ver-tices 1,3,2, whereas the shortest path from s to t2 has cost 7 via vertices 1,3,4,5. Hence 

|SSSP (G, (s, t 1)) вИТ SSSP (G, (s, t 2)) | = 5 вЙ• 1. For (2) we consider an additional edge-weighted graph (H, w H ), also of order six with V (H) := [6] . The graphs G and H have different costs for minimal spanning trees, yet they are indistinguishable by 1-WL . The edge set of 

H is E(H) := {(1 ,2) , (2 ,4) , (4 ,6) , (6 ,5) , (5 ,3) , (3 ,1) , (3 ,4) }, with edge weights wH (1 ,2) := 3 ,

wH (2 ,4) := 1 , wH (4 ,6) := 1 , wH (6 ,5) := 3 , wH (5 ,3) := 1 , wH (3 ,1) := 1 , and wH (3 ,4) := 5 .Hence, the graph H consists of a 6-cycle with a chord of large weight. Observe that MST (G) = 9 .However, in H, because we do not have to include the heavy chord in an MST, we get 

MST (H) = 7 .We now observe that 1-WL , taking edge weights into account, cannot distinguish the graphs G

and H, and it also cannot distinguish t1 and t2 in G. Indeed, in view of the characterization of 1-

WL -distinguishability in terms of unrollings (see Lemma 81), one can verify that unr (G, t 1, L ) = 

unr (G, t 2, L ) for all L. Similarly, there is a bijection ѕА : V (G) вЖТ V (H) such that for all 

v вИИ V (G), unr (G, v, L ) = unr (H, ѕА (v), L ) for all L. Hence, by Morris et al. [75 , Theorem 1 ] , no vertex-level MPNN can separate t1 from t2 on G, and no graph-level MPNN can separate G

from H, implying (1) and (2). 67 For (3), we exhibit two connected graphs G and H such that 1-iWL does not distinguish them (regarding a suitable choice of roots), yet MST (G)ћЄ = MST (H). In both graphs, the vertex set is V (G) = V (H) := [14] , where 7 and 8 are two bridge endpoints and both graphs contain the edge (7 ,8) . The edge sets are 

E(G) := {(1 ,2) , (1 ,3) , (2 ,3) } вИ™ { (4 ,5) , (4 ,6) , (5 ,6) } вИ™ { (3 ,4) }вИ™ { (9 ,10) , (9 ,11) , (10 ,11) } вИ™ { (12 ,13) , (12 ,14) , (13 ,14) } вИ™ { (11 ,12) }вИ™ { (7 , i ) | i вИИ [6] } вИ™ { (8 , j ) | j вИИ { 9,10 ,11 ,12 ,13 ,14 }} вИ™ { (7 ,8) },

and

The weights are defined by wG(3 ,4) = wG(11 ,12) := 5 , wG(7 , i ) := 10 for i вИИ [6] , wG(8 , j ) := 10 

for j вИИ { 9, . . . , 14 }, and wG(e) := 1 for all other e вИИ E(G) (in particular wG(7 ,8) = 1 ); analogously, wH (3 ,4) = wH (11 ,12) := 5 , wH (7 , i ) := 10 for i вИИ [6] , wH (8 , j ) := 10 for j вИИ{9, . . . , 14 }, and wH (e) := 1 for all other e вИИ E(H) (again wH (7 ,8) = 1 ). It is readily verified that MST (G) = 39 and MST (H) = 31 .Let v and w be vertex 1 in G and H, respectively. Then G and H are 1-iWL -indistinguishable regarding v and w. Indeed, this follows from the corresponding characterization in terms of unrollings (see Lemma 81) and the existence of a bijection ѕА : V (G) вЖТ V (H) such that for all 

vвА≤ вИИ V (G), unr (G, v вА≤, L, v ) = unr (H, ѕА (vвА≤), L, w ). Hence, by the same argument as Morris et al. [75 , Theorem 1 ] , no 1-iWL -simulating graph-level MPNN can separate G from H, which implies (3). Now, the following result shows that 1-iWL - and (1 ,1) -WL -simulating MPNNs can arbitrarily well approximate the costs of SSSP and MST, respectively. # F. Expressivity limitations 

This appendix provides additional technical background for Section E.1, including the formal proofs of Lemma 76 and Lemma 77. 69 F.1. Separation and approximation 

We briefly recall known connections between discrete separation power and continuous approxi-mation power , following Azizian and Lelarge [3] and Geerts and Reutter [32]. Let C вКЖ R be compact. For n вИИ N, let Gn,C denote the set of edge-weighted n-order graphs with edge weights in C. One can represent elements in Gn,C by their weighted adjacency matrices in Cn√Чn. Then, equipped with the product topology, Gn,C is compact. Let k вИИ N and let F be a class of continuous functions on Vk(Gn,C ) of the form f : Vk(Gn,C ) вЖТ

RвДУf for some вДУf вИИ N that may depend on f . We write F for the closure of F using the sup norm. That is, a function h : Vk(Gn,C ) вЖТ RвДУh is in F if there exists a sequence (fi)iвЙ•1 вКЖ F 

with fi : Vk(Gn,C ) вЖТ RвДУh such that 

вИ•fi вИТ hвИ•вИЮ := sup   

> (G, v)вИИVk(Gn,C )

вИ•fi(G, v) вИТ h(G, v)вИ•2 вЖТ 0.

We assume F satisfies the following natural assumptions: 1. Concatenation-closed: if f1 : Vk(Gn,C ) вЖТ Rd and f2 : Vk(Gn,C ) вЖТ Rp are in F, then 

G 7 вЖТ (f1(G), f 2(G)) вИИ Rd+p is in F.2. Function/FNN-closed for вДУ: if f : Vk(Gn,C ) вЖТ Rp is in F and g : Rp вЖТ RвДУ is continuous or is an FNN, then g вЧ¶ f вИИ F .For such F we denote by FвДУ the subset of functions in F of the form Vk(Gn,C ) вЖТ RвДУ, i.e., with output dimension fixed to вДУ.Based on a generalized StoneвАУWeierstrass theorem [ 105 ], Geerts and Reutter [32 , Theorem 6.1 ]and Azizian and Lelarge [3, Lemma 32] combined prove the following characterization. 

Theorem 79. Let C вКЖ R be compact, and let n, m, k, вДУ вИИ N. Let F be a class of functions on 

Vk(Gn,C ) that is concatenation-closed and function/FNN-closed for вДУ. Then 

FвДУ = {f : k(Gn,C ) вЖТ RвДУ вИ£вИ£ ѕБVk (Gn,c )(F) вКЖ ѕБVk (Gn,C )(f )}.

Let us fix L вИИ N and consider 

F := вЛГ

> p

MPNN QL

> (TL,p )

(Gn,C ),

i.e., the class of all L-layer graph-level MPNNs as defined in Section 2.2, with TL implemented by FNNs. Fix вДУ вИИ N. As the class F of MPNNs is easily verified to be concatenation-closed and FNN-closed for вДУ, Theorem 79 applies. 

Corollary 80. Let g : Gn,C вЖТ RвДУ be a continuous graph invariant such that g cannot separate more graphs than the class F of L-layer graph-level MPNNs. Then, for any ѕµ > 0, there is an 

L-layer graph-level MPNN f in MPNN QL

> (TL,p )

(Gn,вДУ ) satisfying

> GвИИG n,C

вИ•g(G) вИТ f (G)вИ•2 вЙ§ ѕµ. 

One can obtain similar statements for invariants g : V1(Gn,C ) вЖТ RвДУ and the class F of vertex-level L-layer MPNNs, g : V2(Gn,c ) вЖТ RвДУ and the class F of vertex-level L-layer MPNNs where each (G, (r, v )) вИИ V2(Gn,C ) as a vertex v in G in which r is individualized. 70 In particular, given any alg вИИ { 1-WL ,1 -iWL ,(1,1) -WL } and class of MPNNs that are alg -simulating, the Corollary implies that this class of MPNNs can arbitrarily approximate any invariant g that is upper bounded in expressive power by alg , resulting in Proposition 1 in the main paper. In the following, we will use the above result to shed some light on the abilities of MPNNs to approximate or not be able to approximate invariants corresponding to well-known graph problems. 

F.2. Additional details for Section 4.1 

We first recall the unrolling-tree characterization of 1-WL for vertex-labeled, edge-weighted graphs [76] and then adapt it to characterize 1-iWL as well. Given a connected vertex-labeled and edge-weighted graph (G, вДУ G, w G), we define the unrolling tree of depth L вИИ N0 rooted at a vertex u вИИ V (G), denoted unr (G, u, L ), inductively as follows. 1. For L = 0 , unr (G, u, 0) is the single-vertex tree whose root is labeled вДУG(u).2. For L > 0, unr (G, u, L ) has a root labeled вДУG(u) and, for each neighbor v вИИ N (u), it has a child subtree isomorphic to unr (G, v, L вИТ 1) , connected to the root by an edge of weight 

wG(u, v ).We now extend this notion to incorporate the distinguished root vertex used in 1-iWL . Given a connected vertex-labeled and edge-weighted graph (G, вДУ G, w G) and a fixed vertex r вИИ V (G),we mark r by setting вДУG(r) := [вИЧ]. The unrolling tree regarding r of depth L вИИ N0 rooted at 

u вИИ V (G), denoted unr (G, u, L, r ), is defined inductively as follows. 1. For L = 0 , unr (G, u, 0, r ) is the single-vertex tree whose root is labeled вДУG(u).2. For L > 0, unr (G, u, L, r ) has a root labeled вДУG(u) and, for each neighbor v вИИ N (u), it has a child subtree isomorphic to unr (G, v, L вИТ 1, r ), connected to the root by an edge of weight wG(u, v ).The following lemma is immediate. 

Lemma 81 (Follows from Morris et al. [76 , Lemma 12 ]). The following characterizations hold. 1. For L вИИ N0, given a connected vertex-labeled, edge-weighted graph (G, вДУ G, w G) and vertices 

u, v вИИ V (G), the following are equivalent. 

вАҐ The vertices u and v have the same color after L iterations of 1-WL .

вАҐ The unrolling trees unr (G, u, L ) and unr (G, v, L ) are isomorphic (as rooted, labeled, edge-weighted trees). 2. Claim 83. Let (G, w G) and (H, w H ) be edge-weighted graphs that are (1 ,1) -WL indistinguishable. Then W (G) = W (H). Assume that W (G) = {w1, . . . , w m} with w1 < w 2 < ¬Ј ¬Ј ¬Ј < w m. Then for every j вИИ [m],

# cc (G) = # cc (H). Applying Lemma 82 the suffices to conclude MST (G) = MST (H), as desired. 73 G. MPNN classes satisfying finite Lipschitzness

Figure 5: A compact space of computation trees enables algorithmic generalization. Under an appropriate metric on T , the computation trees Tsmall and Tlarge are close, so regularization-induced Lipschitz continuity of the model implies that good performance on the training instance Tsmall transfers to good performance on the nearby instance

Below, we describe hypothesis classes for graph learning modelsвАФwhich capture many well-known graph algorithmsвАФthat satisfy the finite Lipschitz learning property introduced in Definition 2 and, consequently, Theorem 3. Throughout, the input space X consists of pairs of graphs and vertices belonging to these graphs. The parameter space ќШ is a subset of RP

for some P вИИ N, which will be specified later. We equip the input space with a suitable pseudo-metric such that the conditions of Definition 2 are satisfied. More precisely, we first establish this property for message passing neural networks (MPNNs) with normalized sum aggregation. This result largely follows existing work (see, e.g., [ 93 , 35 , 10 ]) based on iterated degree measure spaces, a continuous counter part of computation trees (see Section K), endowed with the Kantorovich distance (see Section H), but with important simplifications. In particular, in our setting, it is not necessary to show compactness of the input space, since Definition 2 requires only a weaker condition, namely finiteness of the covering number. We then extend the result to MPNNs with mean aggregation. Finally, using similar tools but working on different topological spacesвАФnamely, Hausdorff spaces instead of measure spaces, equipped with the Hausdorff distanceвАФwe show that MPNNs with max and min aggregation satisfy the finite Lipschitz learning property. These architectures capture many commonly used graph algorithms, including shortest-path algorithms, minimum spanning tree problems, and related tasks. Below, we present the precise architectures and 74 state the main result. Overall, we get the following results.
