The following result shows that all the above-defined families of MPNNs satisfied the finite Lipschitz property from Definition 2. 

Theorem 84 (Theorem 4 in the main text) . The hypothesis class FќШ induced by MPNNs using any one of the following aggregation schemes, normalized sum aggregation (Equation (21) ), mean aggregation (Equation (22) ), or max (or min) aggregation (Equation (23) ), satisfies Definition 2. Proof. see Section I, Section J. 

Remark The above result extends directly to graph-level representations. Indeed, if the readout function ѕИ is Lipschitz, then the composition of the vertex-level MPNN with ѕИ remains Lipschitz with respect to the induced graph-level pseudo-metric. Consequently, the finite Lipschitz learning property continues to hold for graph-level prediction tasks. 

Extensions to mixed aggregation architectures The exclusivity of the aggregation choices in Theorem 4 reflects a technical limitation of the proof strategy, i.e., the pseudometrics introduced in Sections I and J are tailored to individual aggregation mechanisms and do not suffice to establish the result simultaneously for all of them, nor for architectures that combine multiple aggregation operators. Nevertheless, the proof techniques developed in Sections I and J extend beyond the specific aggregation schemes considered there. By equipping the relevant input spaces with product topologies endowed with summed metrics, one can naturally combine different aggregation mechanisms, such as mean or normalized sum, together with max (or min), within a single GNN architecture. More generally, the arguments can be unified by considering a generic aggregation operator and defining suitable pseudometrics on the associated iterated neighborhood spaces, such as the KantorovichвАУRubinshtein distance on spaces of iterated degree measures or the Hausdorff distance on spaces of iterated neighborhood sets. With these choices, the input space remains compact, and both aggregation and update maps remain Lipschitz with respect to the resulting metrics. 76 H. Topological, measure-theoretic, and geometric background 

This appendix collects all background material required for the proof of Theorem 4. The central objective is to construct suitable metric spaces for vertex and neighborhood representations induced by message passing neural networks, and to show that these spaces are compact. Compactness will later imply the finiteness of covering numbers, which is the key condition in Definition 2. The appendix is organized as follows. Section H.1 recalls basic notions from topology and metric geometry. Section H.2 reviews measure-theoretic preliminaries and weak вИЧ convergence. Section H.3 introduces iterated degree measures and the KantorovichвАУRubinshtein metric, which underpins the analysis of sum and mean aggregation. Section H.4 develops the hyperspace and Hausdorff-metric framework needed for max (and min) aggregation. 

H.1. Topological background 

We begin by recalling standard definitions from topology, emphasizing compactness and product constructions, which will be repeatedly used in later proofs. 

Definition 85 (Topological space) . A topological space is a pair (X , ѕД ), where X is a set and 

ѕД is a collection of subsets of X containing the empty set and X , closed under arbitrary unions and finite intersections. The elements of ѕД are called open sets. Let (X , ѕД X ) and (Y, ѕД Y ) be topological spaces. A map f : X вЖТ Y is said to be continuous if for every open set U вКЖ Y , the preimage f вИТ1(U ) is an open subset of X .Let ѕД1 and ѕД2 be two topologies on the same set X . We say that ѕД1 is coarser than ѕД2 if 

ѕД1 вКЖ ѕД2.

Definition 86 (Product topology) . Let {(Xi, ѕД i)}iвИИI be a family of topological spaces. The product topology on вИП 

> iвИИI

Xi is the coarsest topology for which all coordinate projections are continuous. 

Definition 87 (Compact space) . A topological space (X , ѕД ) is compact if every open cover of 

X admits a finite subcover. 

Theorem 88 (Tychonoff) . The product of compact topological spaces is compact with respect to the product topology. 

Definition 89 (Metric space and induced topology) . Let X be a set. A function d : X √Ч X вЖТ

[0 , +вИЮ) is a metric on X if, for all x, y, z вИИ X,

d(x, y ) = 0 вЗРвЗТ x = y, d(x, y ) = d(y, x ), d(x, z ) вЙ§ d(x, y ) + d(y, z ).

The pair (X, d ) is called a metric space. The metric d induces a topology on X whose open sets are those U вКВ X such that for every x вИИ U there exists ќµ > 0 with Bd(x, ќµ ) вКВ U .

Definition 90 (Metrizable topology) . A topological space (X , ѕД ) is said to be metrizable if there exists a metric d on X such that the topology induced by d coincides with ѕД .77 H.2. Measure-theoretic background 

We next recall basic measure-theoretic notions that will be used to model neighborhood aggregation by averaging or summation. Let X be a set. A collection A вКЖ 2X is a ѕГ-algebra if X вИИ A , A is closed under complements, and under countable unions. 

Definition 91 (Measure) . Let (X , A) be a measurable space. A measure on (X , A) is a map

Given a topological space (X , ѕД ), we denote by B(X ) = ѕГ(ѕД ) the Borel ѕГ-algebra on X , by MвЙ§1(X ) the space of finite Borel measures with total mass at most 1, and by P(X ) the space of finite Borel measures with total mass exactly 1 (i.e., probability measures). 

Definition 92 (Weak вИЧ topology) . Let (X , ѕД ) be a topological space. The weak вИЧ topology on MвЙ§1(X ) (or similarly on P(X )) is the coarsest topology such that, for every bounded continuous function f : X вЖТ R, the map

Remark 96 . When ќЉ and ќљ are probability measures on X , the KantorovichвАУRubinshtein distance coincides with the 1-Wasserstein distance and admits an equivalent formulation as an optimal transportation problem (see [ 15 ]). The restriction to bounded test functions in the supremum is essential for well-definedness when considering finite (sub-probability) measures. Indeed, without imposing a bound on f , every constant function f вЙ° c is 1-Lipschitz and if 

ќЉ(X )ћЄ = ќљ(X ), then вИЂ

Theorem 97. For every L вИИ N, (HL, d L) is a compact metric space. Proof. We prove the theorem by induction on t. We begin by stating four claims that will be used throughout the proof. 

Claim 1. If (X , d ) is a compact metric space, then MвЙ§1(X ) is compact with respect to the weak вИЧ topology. This follows from Theorem 93. 

Claim 2. If (X , d ) is a compact metric space, then the KantorovichвАУRubinstein metric K

defined in Definition 95 metrizes the weak вИЧ topology on MвЙ§1(X ). This follows from Bogachev [8][Theorem 8.3.2]. 

Claim 3. If A and B are compact topological spaces, then A √Ч B is compact with respect to the product topology. This is a direct consequence of TychonoffвАЩs theorem (Theorem 88). 

Claim 4. Let (A, d A) and (B, d B ) be metric spaces, and define the sum metric d on A √Ч B by 

d((a, b ), (aвА≤, b вА≤)) := dA(a, a вА≤) + dB (b, b вА≤).

K on Mt. Therefore, (Ht, d K,t ) is compact, completing the induction.

# I. Proof of Theorem 4 for sum/mean-aggregation 

This section proves Theorem 4 for the normalized-sum and mean aggregation schemes. The overall strategy is to construct, for each (G, u ) вИИ G r,p 0 вКЧ V , a canonical iterated degree measure 

(IDM) representation in the compact metric space (HL, d K,L ) from Appendix H. This allows us to endow Gr,p 0 вКЧ V with a pseudo-metric induced by dK,L and to deduce finiteness of covering numbers from compactness. We then show that the MPNN maps are Lipschitz with respect to these pseudo-metrics, completing the verification of Definition 2 for the two aggregation schemes. 

Roadmap We proceed in three steps: 1. In Section I.1, we define the induced IDM maps and the corresponding pseudo-metrics on 

V1(Gr,p 0 ).2. In Section I.2, we prove Lipschitz continuity of the normalized-sum and mean MPNNs with respect to the induced pseudo-metrics (via the equivalent IDM formulations). 3. In Section I.3, we state the main conclusion (Theorem 5 in the main paper) and explain how it follows immediately from the Lipschitz bounds and compactness/covering arguments.

81 I.1. Induced IDMs and pseudo-metrics 

In this subsection, we construct mappings from the space V1(Gr,p 0 ) to the space of iterated degree measures (IDMs) HL. This allows us to endow V1(Gr,p 0 ) with a pseudo-metric defined as the recursive KantorovichвАУRubinshtein metric on HL, evaluated on the induced elements. Using the compactness result proved in Appendix H (in particular, Theorem 97), we will later deduce finiteness of the covering number of V1(Gr,p 0 ) with respect to this pseudo-metric. Moreover, we aim to construct these mappings in such a way that message passing neural networks (MPNNs) are Lipschitz continuous with respect to the induced pseudo-metric. For this reason, we introduce different mappings from V1(Gr,p 0 ) to HL, depending on the aggregation scheme under consideration (normalized-sum or mean). We therefore define below two different induced IDMs, one for each aggregation scheme. For this section, without loss of generality we assume that for all G вИИ G r,p 0 , the edge weights wuv вИИ E(0 ,1] for all {u, v } вИИ E(G) (i.e., E вКВ (0 ,1]

# J. Proof of Theorem 4 for max/min-aggregation 

In this section, we prove Theorem 4 for max-aggregation MPNNs. We work in the slightly more general setting where edge weights are vectors in a compact set E вКВ Rde , for de вИИ N; the result in the main paper corresponds to the special case de = 1 . As in the sum/mean case, the proof proceeds by constructing a canonical representation space endowed with a compact metric and showing that the MPNN maps are Lipschitz with respect to the induced pseudo-metric on 

V1(Gr,p 0 ).

Roadmap 

The argument follows the same high-level structure as for sum/mean aggregation: 1. We introduce induced iterated neighborhood-set objects that encode rooted graph neigh-borhoods recursively. 2. We define an induced pseudo-metric on V1(Gr,p 0 ) via a recursive Hausdorff metric. 86 3. We show that max-aggregation MPNNs are Lipschitz with respect to this pseudo-metric. 4. We conclude by combining Lipschitzness with compactness to verify Definition 2.

# K. Iterated degree measures as computation trees 

Computation trees or unrolling trees (see Section F.2) provide a convenient way to understand how message passing neural networks (MPNNs) propagate and aggregate information across a graph. Starting from a root vertex, a computation tree records its neighbors, the neighbors of those neighbors, and so on, up to a prescribed depth. Since MPNNs update vertex repre-sentations by repeatedly aggregating information from local neighborhoods, such trees encode precisely the information required to compute vertex features layer by layer. Iterated degree measures (IDMs) can be viewed as a measure-theoretic analogue of these computation trees. Instead of explicitly storing finite sets of neighbors, IDMs represent neighborhoods as probability measures and neighborhoods of neighborhoods as measures over measures, recursively. This abstraction is particularly natural in the setting of graphons, which are continuous objects extending finite graphs. Formally, a graphon is a measurable function 

W : [0 ,1] 2 вЖТ [0 ,1] , which can be interpreted as a weighted graph with an uncountable vertex set [0 ,1] , where the edge weight between two vertices x, y вИИ [0 ,1] is given by W (x, y ).In a graphon, each vertex has infinitely many neighbors, and its neighborhood cannot be described by a finite multiset. Instead, the local structure around a vertex is naturally captured by a measure encoding the distribution of its neighbors and their attributes. Since edge weights are integrable, these neighborhood measures have total mass at most one. Iterating this constructionвАФtaking measures of neighborhood measuresвАФyields exactly the hierarchy of spaces {MвДУ}LвДУ=0 and {HвДУ}LвДУ=0 introduced above. In this sense, IDMs serve as computation trees for graphons, providing a compact, recursive representation of increasingly deep neighborhood information. This perspective aligns with recent work connecting graph neural networks applied on graphons, and measure-based representations of local structure (see, e.g., [ 35 ]). See also [ 10 ], for a definition of the 1-WL algorithm applied on graphons based on the above analysis. 

# L. Experimental study 

In the following, we outline details related to the experimental evaluation of Q1 to Q3 in Section 6. In addition, we provide further results on size generalization and training set construction. The source code for all experiments is available in the supplementary material. 90 Table 3: Parameters for generation of Erd≈СsвАУReyni and stochastic block model (SBM) graphs in Q1 to Q3 .

Dataset creation To investigate Q1 , we construct multiple training and test datasets. Through-out the experiments, we consider two training sets of graphs. Building on theoretical results on minimal training samples for the SSSP problem, we construct a training set of the minimum number of path graphs, as outlined in Section 4. This results in a set of three graphs for a BellmanвАУFord problem with two steps. In addition, we construct an extended training set to help with training in our MPNN setting. For this, we consider additional path graphs constructed in the same way as for the minimal training set with edge weight x, but with edges scaled by 0.5 and 2. Moreover, we add a special version of the path graph, which includes multiple paths to the furthest reachable vertex. In all training datasets, the initial BellmanвАУFord state and edge weights are given. Moreover, the initial starting vertex is marked with 0 unless otherwise specified. For all experiments, we set the edge weight x = 50 and the value indicating unvisited vertices to 1000 . In addition, all graphs in both the training and test datasets have a self-loop edge for each vertex. As the simplest case of size generalization, we provide a test set, which we call ER-constdeg ,of 200 randomly generated Erd≈СsвАУR√©nyi graphs with an average vertex degree of 6.4. We fix the average vertex degree and increase the number of vertices, generating one dataset for each from 64 to 1024 vertices. Furthermore, we provide a second dataset generated from Erd√ґsвАУReyni graphs, but with an unbounded average degree. For this, we set p = 0 .1 as the edge probability in the graph generator provided by NetworkX [ 39 ]. We call this dataset ER . Again, we provide a test dataset for 64 to 1024 vertices each. As the most general case of the test set, we provide a set of 50 graphs, each consisting of Erd√ґsвАУReyni graphs with unbounded degree, stochastic block model graphs with probability matrices outlined in Table 3, complete graphs as well as star graphs and path graphs as shown in Figure 4. This dataset is called General . Across all test datasets, the weight distribution is uniform on [1 ,100] for all graphs. For Q2 and Q3 , we consider the same training and test sets as in Q1 . However, we restrict our evaluation to a subset of the test datasets, since Q1 showed that the General and ER 

test sets are sufficient for evaluating generalization capabilities. 

Neural architectures The MPNN architecture discussed in Section 4 consists of an update and aggregation function mapping vertex and edge features to an intermediate representation. The ReLU activation function is used, except for the last layer, as this resulted in unstable training behavior. Moreover, all functions in each layer are implemented using a two-layer FNN with the configurations outlined in the following paragraph. 

Hyperparameters and hardware Throughout all experiments, we trained the MPNN archi-tectures for a total of 160000 steps with a learning rate of 0.001 . For size generalization and regularization experiments in Q1 and Q3 , we used a two-layer MPNN as outlined in Section 4. For all experiments, we used the design outlined in the theory of Section 4. In addition, we set the update and aggregation functions to two-layer MLPs with a hidden dimension of 64. The first aggregation FNN uses minimum aggregation with an output dimension of 16, while the second layer reduces the output dimension to 1. All layers are randomly initialized via the uniform initialization provided by PyTorch [86]. Furthermore, we report the runtime and memory usage of our experiments in Table 5. We provide a PyTorch Geometric implementation for each model. All our experiments were executed on a system with 12 CPU cores, an Nvidia L40 GPU, and 120GB of memory. 

Experimental protocol In all experiments, we use the BellmanвАУFord state of the vertices and the edge weights from the graph construction as input to our model. Furthermore, the target is given by the result obtained from BellmanвАУFord after K additional steps from the starting iteration. To calculate the training loss, a combined loss consisting of an вДУ1-loss Lemp and a regularization term Lreg is given: 

L = Lemp + ќЈLreg .

Throughout Q1 , this loss is used to train the model with the regularization outlined in Section 4. For ќЈ, a value of 0.1 is used across experiments. The test score, however, is computed slightly 92 differently. Given the precision hv of a vertex value after K additional BellmanвАУFord steps and the underlying ground truth xv the test score is computed as follows: 

Ltest = 1

|Gtest |

> вИС
> vвИИGtest

|hv вИТ xv|

(xv + 1) .

Therefore, a lower test score implies better generalization by the model to the unseen test dataset. For Q2 , we use slightly modified training and test sets compared to Q1 and Q3 . Since the more expressive MPNN architecture from Section 4 requires a marked starting vertex for BellmanвАУFord, we remove this special label from the training and test data and only provide the BellmanвАУFord initialization from the execution of the algorithm. Otherwise, the parameters and training/test data remain the same as in Q1 .Finally, in Q3 , we consider the regularization term introduced in Section 4 and whether it improves performance over standard вДУ1 or вДУ2 regularization. To conduct the experiment, we select the ER and General datasets outlined in Section L for evaluating both regularization terms. To provide a fair comparison, both architectures were kept the same as in Q1 and executed with the same seeds across experiments. 

Additional results To supplement the results for Q1 , we present detailed results in Figure 7, highlighting similar test behavior across test graph sizes. Furthermore, we provide an additional training dataset containing both random graphs and the minimum-path graphs for the training outlined in Section 4. Moreover, we provide distributions of the weight matrices for Q1 . We omit showing bias value distributions, as they converge to 0 across all FNN layers. In addition, the weight matrices for update MLP layers contain singular non-zero values, with most entries remaining zero during training. Similar results can be observed for the aggregation FNN layers. Furthermore, the singular non-zero weights converge to positive values across all layers, with a maximum of 1.5 observed in the aggregation FNN of the first MPNN layer. We observe similar behavior for вДУ1 and вДУ2 regularization, but not as pronounced as in Section L. Since size generalization experiments in Q1 were conducted only for two BellmanвАУFord steps, we aim to provide additional insights into predicting future BellmanвАУFord steps. Using the setup from Q1 , we predict three steps of BellmanвАУFord instead of two. For this, we use the same MPNN as in Q1 and highlight the results obtained in Table 7. We note that a two-layer MPNN is not sufficient to learn to predict three steps of BellmanвАУFord from scratch with the given training set from Q1 . However, this aligns with theoretical results, indicating that at least one layer is needed for each step of BellmanвАУFord to be sufficiently predicted. Finally, Table 8 presents additional results for Q2 , highlighting differences between training and test results arising from the increased expressivity of the 1-iWL -MPNN. As shown, the training loss and test score are significantly higher for Q2 than for the MPNN in Q1 . This aligns empirical results with theoretical observations on the required expressivity for the SSSP problem, as seen in Proposition 6. 93 Table 6: Additional results for the ER-constdeg and ER dataset used for size generalization in Q1 .
